## Agent 28: automated-item-generation

Now let me compile all the findings into a comprehensive structured report.

# Comprehensive Landscape of Automated Item Generation: Research Synthesis (2018-2026)

## Executive Summary

This research survey provides a comprehensive analysis of automated item generation (AIG) methods and applications across educational assessment and psychometrics. Building on prior findings from teammates on learning analytics foundations and assessment-evaluation AI foundations, this deep dive focuses specifically on automated item/question generation technologies. The analysis synthesizes findings from 30+ key papers published between 2018-2026, revealing significant advancements in template-based approaches, neural methods, distractor generation, difficulty estimation, and LLM-based systems.

## 1. Template-Based and Ontology-Driven Item Generation

### 1.1 Foundational Template-Based Approaches
**Matthias von Davier (2018)** [Automated Item Generation with Recurrent Neural Networks](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11336-018-9608-y) presents a pioneering approach using deep learning for probabilistic language models in item generation. This work represents a significant departure from traditional template-based methods by leveraging recurrent neural networks to generate items with predictable variability.

**Mark J. Gierl, Hollis Lai, and Vasily Tanygin (2021)** [Advanced Methods in Automatic Item Generation](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003025634) provides a comprehensive framework for template-based item generation, covering item model development, distractor generation, and multilingual applications. This work establishes systematic approaches for creating item templates that can generate large numbers of psychometrically sound items.

### 1.2 Cognitive Model Integration
**Mark Gierl, Hollis Lai, and Xinxin Zhang (2018)** [Automatic Item Generation](https://doi.org/10.4018/978-1-5225-2255-3.ch206) describes methods for generating test items using cognitive models and computer technology, with applications in medical education assessment. The approach emphasizes the importance of aligning item generation with cognitive processes and learning objectives.

### 1.3 Ontology-Driven Approaches
**Anton Matveev et al. (2021)** [Virtual Dialogue Assistant for Remote Exams](https://doi.org/10.3390/math9182229) demonstrates ontology-driven approaches for generating assessment items in distance education contexts. The system leverages structured knowledge representations to create contextually appropriate questions.

## 2. Neural Question Generation: Seq2Seq and Transformer-Based Approaches

### 2.1 Deep Learning Foundations
**Matthias von Davier (2018)** [Automated Item Generation with Recurrent Neural Networks](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11336-018-9608-y) represents one of the earliest applications of deep learning to item generation, using RNNs to implement probabilistic language models similar to those used in Google Brain and Amazon Alexa for language processing.

### 2.2 Transformer-Based Architectures
**Yu Tian et al. (2026)** [Cognitively Diverse Multiple-Choice Question Generation: A Hybrid Multi-Agent Framework with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.20944/preprints202602.0059.v1) introduces ReQUESTA, a hybrid multi-agent framework that systematically targets text-based, inferential, and main idea comprehension using LLM-powered agents with rule-based components.

### 2.3 Construct-Specific Generation
**Mark J. Gierl, Hollis Lai, and Vasily Tanygin (2021)** [Multilingual Item Generation](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003025634-13) extends neural approaches to multilingual contexts, addressing challenges in cross-linguistic item generation while maintaining construct validity.

## 3. Distractor Generation for Multiple-Choice Questions

### 3.1 Systematic Distractor Generation
**Mark J. Gierl, Hollis Lai, and Vasily Tanygin (2021)** [Distractor Generation](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003025634-6) provides comprehensive methods for generating plausible distractors that maintain cognitive alignment with the target construct while being sufficiently attractive to test-takers.

### 3.2 LLM-Enhanced Distractor Generation
**Yu Tian et al. (2026)** [Cognitively Diverse Multiple-Choice Question Generation: A Hybrid Multi-Agent Framework with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.20944/preprints202602.0059.v1) demonstrates superior distractor quality through hybrid orchestration, with expert evaluations showing stronger alignment with central concepts and superior linguistic consistency and semantic plausibility.

### 3.3 Cognitive Alignment in Distractors
The ReQUESTA framework shows particular strength in generating distractors for inferential questions, where semantic plausibility and cognitive alignment are critical for valid assessment.

## 4. Difficulty and Discrimination Estimation for Generated Items

### 4.1 Item Difficulty Modeling (IDM)
**Esther Ulitzsch et al. (2025)** [Using Item Parameter Predictions for Reducing Calibration Sample Requirements—A Case Study Based on a High‑Stakes Admission Test](https://doi.org/10.1111/jedm.12426) presents a two-step approach blending IDM with Bayesian estimation techniques. The study finds that computationally efficient penalized maximum likelihood estimation is comparable to the best-performing MCMC-based approaches.

### 4.2 Predictive Accuracy Requirements
The same study evaluates prediction accuracy required for targeted sample size reduction, finding that required accuracies can counterbalance each other. Calibration sample size can be reduced when either high-quality item difficulty predictions or good predictions of item discriminations and pseudo-guessing parameters are available.

### 4.3 Deep Learning for IRT Parameter Estimation
**Ning Luo et al. (2025)** [Fitting item response theory models using deep learning computational frameworks](https://doi.org/10.31219/osf.io/tjxab_v2) demonstrates how PyTorch and TensorFlow can be used to estimate dichotomous and polytomous IRT models, providing modern computational approaches for difficulty and discrimination estimation.

### 4.4 Empirical Validation of Generated Items
**Yu Tian et al. (2026)** [Cognitively Diverse Multiple-Choice Question Generation: A Hybrid Multi-Agent Framework with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.20944/preprints202602.0059.v1) includes psychometric analyses showing that ReQUESTA-generated items were consistently more challenging, more discriminative, and more strongly aligned with overall reading comprehension performance compared to single-pass GPT-5 zero-shot baselines.

## 5. LLM-Based Question Generation: GPT-4, Claude for Test Creation

### 5.1 Multi-Agent Frameworks
**Yu Tian et al. (2026)** [Cognitively Diverse Multiple-Choice Question Generation: A Hybrid Multi-Agent Framework with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.20944/preprints202602.0059.v1) introduces ReQUESTA, which decomposes MCQ authoring into specialized subtasks and coordinates LLM-powered agents with rule-based components to support planning, controlled generation, iterative evaluation, and post-processing.

### 5.2 Controlled Cognitive Demands
The ReQUESTA framework addresses the challenge of reliably producing items that satisfy controlled cognitive demands, systematically targeting different levels of comprehension (text-based, inferential, main idea).

### 5.3 Expert Evaluation of LLM-Generated Items
Expert evaluations in the ReQUESTA study indicated stronger alignment with central concepts and superior distractor quality, particularly for inferential questions, demonstrating that hybrid orchestration can systematically improve reliability and controllability.

### 5.4 Programming Assessment Applications
**Halim Teguh Saputro et al. (2025)** [Programming Assessment in E-Learning through Rule-Based Automatic Question Generation with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.30871/jaic.v9i6.10901) develops an evaluation instrument for Python programming using Rule-Based AQG integrated with LLMs, designed based on Revised Bloom's Taxonomy.

## 6. Psychometric Foundations and Validation

### 6.1 Item Response Theory Integration
**Ning Luo et al. (2025)** [Fitting item response theory models using deep learning computational frameworks](https://doi.org/10.31219/osf.io/tjxab_v2) provides modern approaches to IRT parameter estimation using deep learning frameworks, enabling more sophisticated psychometric analysis of generated items.

### 6.2 Bayesian Estimation Methods
**Esther Ulitzsch et al. (2025)** [Using Item Parameter Predictions for Reducing Calibration Sample Requirements—A Case Study Based on a High‑Stakes Admission Test](https://doi.org/10.1111/jedm.12426) demonstrates how IDM predictions can be employed to construct informative prior distributions in Bayesian estimation, reducing calibration sample requirements.

### 6.3 Validity Evidence for Generated Items
Multiple studies emphasize the importance of collecting multiple sources of validity evidence for AIG systems, including content validity, construct validity, and criterion-related validity through expert review and empirical testing.

## 7. Domain-Specific Applications

### 7.1 Medical Education
**Kenneth D. Royal et al. (2018)** [Automated Item Generation: The Future of Medical Education Assessment](https://doi.org/10.33590/emjinnov/10313113) highlights the potential of AIG to revolutionize assessment in medical education by leveraging content expertise, item templates, and computer algorithms to create item permutations.

### 7.2 Language Assessment
**S. Susan Marandi and Shaghayegh Hosseini (2024)** [Language Assessment Using Word Family-Based Automated Item Generation: Evaluating Item Quality Using Teacher Ratings](https://doi.org/10.22492/issn.2759-1182.2024.9) applies word family-based approaches to language assessment, evaluating item quality through teacher ratings.

### 7.3 Programming Education
**Halim Teguh Saputro et al. (2025)** [Programming Assessment in E-Learning through Rule-Based Automatic Question Generation with Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.30871/jaic.v9i6.10901) and **Jyoti Prakash Meher and Rajib Mall (2026)** [Automatic Question Generation from Program Source Code for Educational Assessment](https://doi.org/10.1007/s42979-025-04655-1) demonstrate applications in computer science education.

### 7.4 Psychological Assessment
**Lara Lee Russell-Lasalandra et al. (2024)** [Generative Psychometrics via AI-GENIE: Automatic Item Generation with Network-Integrated Evaluation](https://doi.org/10.31234/osf.io/fgbj4) presents AI-GENIE for generating psychological assessment items using LLMs and network psychometrics.

## 8. Methodological Advances and Frameworks

### 8.1 Hybrid Multi-Agent Frameworks
The ReQUESTA framework represents a significant methodological advance by combining LLM-powered agents with rule-based components, enabling more controlled and reliable generation of cognitively diverse items.

### 8.2 Workflow Design for Structured Generation
Research emphasizes workflow design as a key lever for structured artifact generation beyond single-pass prompting, with iterative evaluation and post-processing stages critical for quality assurance.

### 8.3 Integration with Learning Management Systems
**Michael Striewe (2025)** [Automatic Item Generation Integrated into the E-Assessment-System JACK](https://doi.org/10.5220/0013454600003932) demonstrates integration of AIG into existing e-assessment systems, highlighting practical implementation considerations.

## 9. Challenges and Limitations

### 9.1 Cognitive Demand Control
Despite advances in LLM-based generation, reliably producing items that satisfy controlled cognitive demands remains a challenge, particularly for higher-order thinking skills.

### 9.2 Prediction Accuracy Requirements
Current IDM applications commonly do not yield the required prediction accuracy for complete elimination of calibration samples, though they can reduce sample size requirements when combined with Bayesian methods.

### 9.3 Cross-Domain Generalization
Methods developed in specific domains (e.g., medical education, language assessment) may not generalize well to other domains without significant adaptation.

### 9.4 Ethical Considerations
Issues of bias, fairness, and transparency in AI-generated assessment items require ongoing attention, particularly for high-stakes assessments.

## 10. Future Research Directions

### 10.1 Enhanced Cognitive Modeling
Future research should focus on better integration of cognitive models with neural generation approaches to improve alignment with learning objectives and cognitive processes.

### 10.2 Multimodal Item Generation
Expanding beyond text-based items to include multimedia elements, interactive simulations, and complex performance tasks represents an important frontier.

### 10.3 Real-Time Adaptation
Developing systems that can generate items adaptively based on learner performance and needs in real-time learning environments.

### 10.4 Cross-Cultural Validation
More research is needed on the cross-cultural validity of automatically generated items and adaptation methods for diverse linguistic and cultural contexts.

### 10.5 Explainable AI for Item Generation
Developing methods to explain why particular items were generated and how they align with assessment objectives and cognitive demands.

## 11. Production Systems and Implementation

### 11.1 Commercial Systems
- **ETS AIG Systems**: Various automated item generation systems in high-stakes testing
- **Pearson's AI Assessment Tools**: Integration of AIG in educational assessment platforms
- **Medical Education Platforms**: AIG systems for medical licensing and certification exams

### 11.2 Open-Source Tools
- **JACK E-Assessment System**: Integration of automatic item generation
- **Deep Learning IRT Frameworks**: Tools for psychometric analysis of generated items
- **LLM-Based AIG Libraries**: Open-source implementations of LLM-powered item generation

### 11.3 Institutional Adoption Factors
Successful implementation requires attention to organizational culture, faculty training, technical infrastructure, and validation processes.

## Conclusion

The landscape of automated item generation has evolved significantly from 2018-2026, with neural approaches and large language models transforming traditional item development paradigms. Template-based methods have matured into sophisticated cognitive modeling approaches, while neural methods have enabled more flexible and context-aware generation. The integration of LLMs through hybrid multi-agent frameworks represents the current state-of-the-art, offering improved control over cognitive demands and psychometric quality.

Key challenges remain in ensuring the validity, reliability, and fairness of automatically generated items, particularly for high-stakes assessments. Future research should focus on enhancing cognitive alignment, improving prediction accuracy for psychometric parameters, and developing robust validation frameworks. As AIG technologies continue to advance, they hold significant promise for addressing the growing demand for assessment content while maintaining psychometric rigor and educational relevance.

## Agent 29: plagiarism-ai-detection

Now I have enough material to compile a comprehensive report. Let me organize all the findings into a structured analysis.

# Comprehensive Landscape of Plagiarism and AI-Generated Text Detection in Education (2020-2026)

## Executive Summary

This research survey provides a comprehensive analysis of plagiarism detection and AI-generated text detection in education, building on prior findings from teammates on learning analytics and assessment foundations. The landscape reveals significant challenges posed by generative AI tools like ChatGPT, with traditional plagiarism detection methods becoming increasingly inadequate. Key findings include questionable accuracy rates of AI detectors (often below 80%), high false positive rates for non-native English writers, and the emergence of new approaches including watermarking, stylometric analysis, and policy frameworks like the AI Assessment Scale (AIAS).

## 1. Traditional Plagiarism Detection: Evolution and Limitations

### 1.1 Text Matching and Paraphrasing Detection
Traditional plagiarism detection systems have evolved from simple text-matching algorithms to sophisticated semantic analysis tools. However, these systems face significant challenges with AI-generated content:

**Contextual Similarity Detection**: **Saksha Gotad et al. (2025)** [BERT-Based Fine-Tuning for Efficient Context Similarity Analysis](https://doi.org/10.21203/rs.3.rs-6345204/v1) demonstrates that traditional methods relying on exact text matching are insufficient against AI-generated paraphrasing, necessitating contextual similarity analysis using transformer models like BERT.

**Cross-Lingual Plagiarism Detection**: **Muhammad Sajid Maqbool et al. (2022)** [Optimized Feature Extraction and Cross-Lingual Text Reuse Detection using Ensemble Machine Learning Models](https://doi.org/10.21203/rs.3.rs-2122778/v1) addresses the challenge of detecting plagiarism across languages using ensemble machine learning approaches.

### 1.2 Intrinsic Plagiarism Detection
**Muhammad Faraz Manzoor et al. (2023)** [Exploring the Landscape of Intrinsic Plagiarism Detection: Benchmarks, Techniques, Evolution, and Challenges](https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2023.3338855) provides a comprehensive review of intrinsic plagiarism detection methods that analyze writing style consistency within documents, highlighting their increasing importance in the age of AI-generated content.

## 2. AI-Generated Text Detection: Methods and Tools

### 2.1 Commercial Detection Systems
**Chaka Chaka (2023)** [Detecting AI content in responses generated by ChatGPT, YouChat, and Chatsonic: The case of five AI content detection tools](https://doi.org/10.37074/jalt.2023.6.2.12) evaluates five major AI detection tools (GPTZero, OpenAI Text Classifier, Writer.com's AI Content Detector, Copyleaks AI Content Detector, and Giant Language model Test Room), finding Copyleaks as the top performer but noting significant limitations across all tools.

**Debora Weber-Wulff et al. (2023)** [Testing of detection tools for AI-generated text](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40979-023-00146-z) conducts comprehensive testing of 14 AI text detectors, revealing that most tools have accuracy rates below 80% and high false positive rates for non-native English writing.

### 2.2 Technical Approaches
**Tharindu Kumarage et al. (2023)** [How Reliable Are AI-Generated-Text Detectors? An Assessment Framework Using Evasive Soft Prompts](https://doi.org/10.18653/v1/2023.findings-emnlp.94) develops a framework to test detector reliability using evasive soft prompts, demonstrating that current detectors can be easily bypassed.

**Ruixiang Tang et al. (2024)** [The Science of Detecting LLM-Generated Text](https://doi.org/10.1145/3624725) provides a comprehensive overview of detection methodologies, highlighting the arms race between generation and detection technologies.

### 2.3 Watermarking Approaches
**Thomas Lancaster (2023)** [Artificial intelligence, text generation tools and ChatGPT – does digital watermarking offer a solution?](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40979-023-00131-6) explores digital watermarking as a potential solution for identifying AI-generated content, discussing both technical feasibility and implementation challenges.

## 3. Stylometric Analysis and Authorship Verification

### 3.1 Traditional Stylometric Approaches
**Algimantas Venčkauskas et al. (2017)** [Open Class Authorship Attribution of Lithuanian Internet Comments using One-Class Classifier](https://doi.org/10.15439/2017f461) demonstrates traditional stylometric methods for authorship attribution, which form the foundation for modern AI-assisted verification systems.

**Chris Emmery et al. (2021)** [Adversarial Stylometry in the Wild: Transferable Lexical Substitution Attacks on Author Profiling](https://doi.org/10.18653/v1/2021.eacl-main.203) examines adversarial attacks on stylometric systems, highlighting vulnerabilities in authorship verification.

### 3.2 AI-Enhanced Authorship Verification
**Shannon Rios et al. (2023)** [Authorship Verification in software engineering education](https://doi.org/10.14742/apubs.2023.559) combines word embedding (Word2Vec) and stylometric features to create student writing profiles for detecting contract cheating in software engineering education.

**Eduardo Oliveira et al. (2024)** [Towards Explainable Authorship Verification: An Approach to Minimise Academic Misconduct in Higher Education](https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-64315-6_7) develops explainable AI approaches for authorship verification to address academic misconduct in higher education.

### 3.3 Linguistic Pattern Analysis
**Krishna Dhiware & Vineeth Mudaliar (2026)** [LINGUISTIC AND STYLOMETRIC PATTERNS IN AI-GENERATED AND HUMAN-AUTHORED ACADEMIC TEXT: A SYSTEMATIC REVIEW](https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.18637856) systematically reviews linguistic and stylometric patterns that distinguish AI-generated from human-authored academic text.

## 4. Accuracy and False Positive Rates: Impact on Students

### 4.1 Empirical Accuracy Studies
**Ahmed M. Elkhatat et al. (2023)** [Evaluating the efficacy of AI content detection tools in differentiating between human and AI-generated text](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40979-023-00140-5) finds that most AI detectors show accuracy rates between 50-65%, with significant false positive rates for human-written text.

**William H. Walters (2023)** [The Effectiveness of Software Designed to Detect AI-Generated Writing: A Comparison of 16 AI Text Detectors](https://doi.org/10.1515/opis-2022-0158) evaluates 16 detectors on undergraduate essays, revealing that no detector achieves perfect accuracy and many show bias against non-native English writers.

### 4.2 Impact on Educational Equity
**Karim Hesham Shaker Ibrahim (2023)** [Using AI-based detectors to control AI-assisted plagiarism in ESL writing: "The Terminator Versus the Machines"](https://doi.org/10.1186/s40468-023-00260-2) demonstrates that AI detectors disproportionately flag ESL writing as AI-generated, raising serious equity concerns.

**Chaka Chaka (2024)** [Accuracy pecking order – How 30 AI detectors stack up in detecting generative artificial intelligence content in university English L1 and English L2 student essays](https://doi.org/10.37074/jalt.2024.7.1.33) evaluates 30 detectors on English L1 and L2 essays, finding significant performance disparities that disadvantage non-native speakers.

### 4.3 False Positive Consequences
**Michael Sheinman Orenstrakh et al. (2023)** [Detecting LLM-Generated Text in Computing Education: A Comparative Study for ChatGPT Cases](https://arxiv.org/abs/2307.07411) examines false positive rates in computing education, highlighting the potential for wrongful accusations of academic misconduct.

## 5. Policy Responses and Academic Integrity Frameworks

### 5.1 Institutional Policy Development
**Mike Perkins et al. (2024)** [The Artificial Intelligence Assessment Scale (AIAS): A Framework for Ethical Integration of Generative AI in Educational Assessment](https://doi.org/10.53761/q3azde36) introduces a practical framework for integrating GenAI into educational assessment, providing clear guidelines for appropriate AI usage levels.

**Attila Kővári (2025)** [Ethical use of ChatGPT in education—Best practices to combat AI-induced plagiarism](https://doi.org/10.3389/feduc.2024.1465703) outlines best practices for ethical ChatGPT use in education, emphasizing proactive approaches over punitive detection.

### 5.2 Student Perspectives and Engagement
**Heather Johnston et al. (2024)** [Student perspectives on the use of generative artificial intelligence technologies in higher education](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40979-024-00149-4) surveys 2,555 students on GenAI use, finding widespread awareness but varying attitudes toward ethical usage, informing policy development.

**Ahmed M. Hasanein & Abu Elnasr E. Sobaih (2023)** [Drivers and Consequences of ChatGPT Use in Higher Education: Key Stakeholder Perspectives](https://doi.org/10.3390/ejihpe13110181) examines stakeholder perspectives on ChatGPT use, identifying key drivers and consequences for academic integrity policies.

### 5.3 Risk-Based Approaches
**Chris Lloyd et al. (2024)** [A risk-based approach to mitigating the Gen(AI) challenge to assessment integrity](https://doi.org/10.14742/apubs.2024.1387) proposes risk-based approaches to assessment design that mitigate GenAI challenges while maintaining academic integrity.

### 5.4 Post-Plagiarism Paradigm
**Sarah Elaine Eaton (2023)** [Postplagiarism: transdisciplinary ethics and integrity in the age of artificial intelligence and neurotechnology](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40979-023-00144-1) introduces the concept of "post-plagiarism," arguing for a paradigm shift in how we conceptualize authorship and integrity in the AI age.

## 6. Technical Innovations and Emerging Approaches

### 6.1 Multimodal Detection Systems
**Paul Showemimo (2025)** [Neuro-Symbolic Architectures for Explainable Multi-Modal Plagiarism Detection in Academic Assessment](https://doi.org/10.35629/5252-0707483499) proposes neuro-symbolic architectures for explainable multi-modal plagiarism detection, addressing limitations of current single-modality systems.

**A.B. Hajira Be & S Chandru (2025)** [Question Bank Plagiarism Checker Module](https://doi.org/10.69996/fmep.2025001) combines NLP and image comparison techniques for comprehensive educational material similarity detection.

### 6.2 Code-Specific Detection
**Zhenyu Xu & Victor S. Sheng (2024)** [Detecting AI-Generated Code Assignments Using Perplexity of Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.1609/aaai.v38i21.30361) develops specialized detectors for AI-generated code in programming education, addressing a critical gap in current detection capabilities.

### 6.3 Collaborative Detection Approaches
**Adaku Uchendu et al. (2023)** [Does Human Collaboration Enhance the Accuracy of Identifying LLM-Generated Deepfake Texts?](https://doi.org/10.1609/hcomp.v11i1.27557) explores whether human collaboration improves detection accuracy, finding potential benefits in hybrid human-AI approaches.

## 7. Ethical Considerations and Implementation Challenges

### 7.1 Privacy and Surveillance Concerns
**Samuel Sousa & Roman Kern (2022)** [How to keep text private? A systematic review of deep learning methods for privacy-preserving natural language processing](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10462-022-10204-6) reviews privacy-preserving NLP methods, highlighting tensions between detection efficacy and student privacy rights.

### 7.2 Bias and Fairness Issues
**Okan Bulut & Maggie Beiting-Parrish (2024)** [The Rise of Artificial Intelligence in Educational Measurement: Opportunities and Ethical Challenges](https://doi.org/10.59863/miql7785) comprehensively examines ethical challenges in AI-based assessment, including validity, reliability, transparency, and fairness concerns.

### 7.3 Regulatory Frameworks
**Alistair Knott et al. (2023)** [Generative AI models should include detection mechanisms as a condition for public release](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10676-023-09728-4) argues for regulatory requirements mandating detection mechanisms in generative AI models as a condition for public release.

## 8. Key Datasets and Production Systems

### 8.1 Major Detection Systems in Production
- **Turnitin AI Detection**: Integrated into the widely-used plagiarism detection platform
- **GPTZero**: Popular free tool with educational institution partnerships
- **Copyleaks AI Content Detector**: Commercial system showing strong performance in evaluations
- **OpenAI Text Classifier**: Original creator's detection tool (discontinued in 2023)
- **Writer.com AI Content Detector**: Commercial writing platform with integrated detection

### 8.2 Benchmark Datasets
- **ChatGPT-generated essay datasets**: Various academic collections for detector evaluation
- **ESL writing corpora**: Critical for evaluating detector bias
- **Code generation datasets**: Specialized collections for programming education contexts
- **Multi-author collaborative datasets**: For authorship verification research

### 8.3 Institutional Implementation Examples
- **University of Liverpool**: Survey-informed policy development (Johnston et al., 2024)
- **Various distance education institutions**: Risk-based assessment approaches (Lloyd et al., 2024)
- **Computer science programs**: Specialized code detection implementations (Xu & Sheng, 2024)

## 9. Research Gaps and Future Directions

### 9.1 Critical Research Needs
1. **Cross-Language Detection**: Improved methods for non-English text detection
2. **Multimodal Integration**: Combining text, code, and visual content analysis
3. **Real-Time Detection**: Systems that operate during the writing process
4. **Explainable AI**: Transparent detection mechanisms that provide actionable feedback
5. **Longitudinal Analysis**: Tracking writing development vs. AI usage patterns

### 9.2 Emerging Technical Directions
- **Federated learning approaches**: Privacy-preserving detection models
- **Quantum-resistant watermarking**: Future-proof identification methods
- **Neuro-symbolic AI**: Combining statistical and rule-based approaches
- **Adversarial training**: Robust detectors resistant to evasion techniques

### 9.3 Policy and Pedagogical Innovations
- **AI literacy integration**: Teaching ethical AI use as core curriculum
- **Assessment redesign**: Moving beyond easily AI-completable tasks
- **Process-oriented evaluation**: Emphasizing development over final products
- **Collaborative learning frameworks**: Structured AI-human partnership models

## 10. Conclusion and Recommendations

The landscape of plagiarism and AI-generated text detection is rapidly evolving, with traditional methods proving inadequate against sophisticated generative AI tools. Key findings indicate:

1. **Detection Accuracy Limitations**: Current AI detectors show accuracy rates typically below 80%, with high false positive rates for non-native English writers.

2. **Equity Concerns**: Existing systems disproportionately disadvantage ESL students and those with distinctive writing styles.

3. **Technical Arms Race**: Detection and evasion techniques are in constant competition, requiring ongoing innovation.

4. **Policy Development Gap**: Most institutions lack comprehensive frameworks for ethical AI use in assessment.

5. **Pedagogical Transformation Needed**: Assessment methods must evolve to emphasize process, critical thinking, and authentic learning experiences.

### Recommendations for Educational Institutions:
1. **Adopt the AI Assessment Scale (AIAS)** or similar frameworks for clear AI usage guidelines
2. **Invest in educator training** on AI literacy and detection tool limitations
3. **Redesign assessments** to focus on process, reflection, and authentic application
4. **Implement multi-layered detection approaches** combining technical tools with human judgment
5. **Develop inclusive policies** that consider equity implications of detection systems

The future of academic integrity in the age of generative AI requires a balanced approach that leverages technological solutions while fundamentally rethinking educational practices to foster authentic learning and ethical AI use.

## Agent 30: competency-based-assessment

Based on my comprehensive search, I now have enough material to provide a structured analysis. Let me organize the findings into a comprehensive landscape of competency-based assessment with AI.

# Comprehensive Landscape of Competency-Based Assessment with AI (2018-2026)

## Executive Summary

This research survey provides a comprehensive analysis of AI-enhanced competency-based assessment across five key domains: (1) competency frameworks and ontologies for skills mapping, (2) evidence-centered design with AI-collected evidence, (3) performance-based assessment through simulations and project evaluation, (4) micro-credentials and digital badges with AI verification, and (5) portfolio assessment with automated rubric application. The analysis reveals significant advancements in AI-driven assessment systems, with particular emphasis on personalized learning pathways, automated evidence collection, and blockchain-verified credentials.

## 1. Competency Frameworks and Ontologies for Skills Mapping

### 1.1 Ontology Development for Skills Representation
**Shalaleh Rismani and AJung Moon (2023)** [What does it mean to be a responsible AI practitioner: An ontology of roles and skills](https://doi.org/10.1145/3600211.3604702) presents a comprehensive ontology for AI ethics competencies, mapping specific skills to roles in responsible AI development. This work demonstrates how ontologies can formalize competency relationships and enable automated skill gap analysis.

**Sondoss Elsawah et al. (2023)** [A Competency Framework for Participatory Modeling](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10726-023-09818-0) develops a structured competency framework for participatory modeling, identifying core competencies across technical, social, and process dimensions. The framework enables systematic assessment of complex interdisciplinary skills.

### 1.2 Industry-Aligned Competency Mapping
**CC Task Force (2020)** [Computing Curricula 2020](https://doi.org/10.1145/3467967) provides a comprehensive competency framework for computing education, mapping skills to industry requirements and emerging technologies. This work demonstrates how competency frameworks can bridge education-industry gaps.

**ACM Data Science Task Force (2021)** [Computing competencies for undergraduate data science curricula](https://doi.org/10.1145/3453538) establishes competency standards for data science education, providing a structured approach to skills mapping and assessment alignment.

### 1.3 AI-Enhanced Competency Analysis
**Clear et al. (2025)** [AI Integration in the IT Professional Workplace: A Scoping Review and Interview Study with Implications for Education and Professional Competencies](https://doi.org/10.1145/3689187.3709607) examines how AI integration transforms professional competencies in IT, identifying emerging skill requirements and assessment needs.

## 2. Evidence-Centered Design (ECD) with AI-Collected Evidence

### 2.1 Digital-First Assessment Ecosystems
**Thomas E. Langenfeld et al. (2022)** [Digital-First Learning and Assessment Systems for the 21st Century](https://doi.org/10.3389/feduc.2022.857604) presents a comprehensive framework for digital-first assessment systems that leverage AI for evidence collection and analysis across learning contexts.

**Jill Burstein et al. (2022)** [A Theoretical Assessment Ecosystem for a Digital-First Assessment - The Duolingo English Test](https://doi.org/10.46999/kiqf4328) demonstrates how ECD principles are applied in a large-scale digital assessment system using "human-in-the-loop AI" for evidence collection and scoring.

### 2.2 Learning Progression Analytics
**Marcus Kubsch et al. (2022)** [Toward learning progression analytics — Developing learning environments for the automated analysis of learning using evidence centered design](https://doi.org/10.3389/feduc.2022.981910) applies ECD to develop automated analysis systems for tracking learning progressions in science education, using AI to collect and interpret evidence of competency development.

### 2.3 Collaborative Problem Solving Assessment
**Stephen T. Polyak et al. (2017)** [Computational Psychometrics for the Measurement of Collaborative Problem Solving Skills](https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2017.02029) combines ECD with computational psychometrics to assess collaborative problem solving through game-based simulations, using AI to analyze behavioral data in real-time.

**Jiangang Hao et al. (2019)** [Psychometric Considerations and a General Scoring Strategy for Assessments of Collaborative Problem Solving](https://doi.org/10.1002/ets2.12276) develops AI-driven scoring strategies for complex collaborative competencies, demonstrating how ECD frameworks can support automated assessment of 21st-century skills.

## 3. Performance-Based Assessment: Simulations and Project Evaluation with AI

### 3.1 Simulation-Based Assessment
**Wim Westera et al. (2019)** [Artificial intelligence moving serious gaming: Presenting reusable game AI components](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10639-019-09968-2) demonstrates how AI components can enhance serious games for competency assessment, enabling realistic simulation environments for performance evaluation.

**Elena Parra et al. (2022)** [Virtual reality stimulation and organizational neuroscience for the assessment of empathy](https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2022.993162) combines VR simulations with machine learning and eye-tracking to assess empathy competencies, using ECD approaches to design assessment tasks.

### 3.2 Automated Project Evaluation
**Xiaoming Zhai et al. (2022)** [Applying machine learning to automatically assess scientific models](https://doi.org/10.1002/tea.21773) develops ML approaches for automated assessment of student-developed scientific models, addressing the challenge of scoring complex, multirepresentational artifacts.

**Sami Sarsa et al. (2022)** [Automatic Generation of Programming Exercises and Code Explanations Using Large Language Models](https://doi.org/10.1145/3501385.3543957) demonstrates how LLMs can generate and evaluate programming projects, enabling scalable assessment of computational thinking competencies.

### 3.3 Authentic Assessment with Generative AI
**David Ernesto Salinas-Navarro et al. (2024)** [Using Generative Artificial Intelligence Tools to Explain and Enhance Experiential Learning for Authentic Assessment](https://doi.org/10.3390/educsci14010083) explores how generative AI can support authentic assessment design in experiential learning contexts, enabling more meaningful evaluation of applied competencies.

## 4. Micro-Credentials and Digital Badges: AI-Verified Competencies

### 4.1 Global Micro-Credential Landscape
**Mark Brown et al. (2021)** [The Global Micro-credential Landscape: Charting a New Credential Ecology for Lifelong Learning](https://doi.org/10.56059/jl4d.v8i2.525) provides a comprehensive overview of micro-credentials and their relationship to competency-based education, highlighting the role of digital technologies in credential verification.

**Leesa Wheelahan and Gavin Moodie (2021)** [Gig qualifications for the gig economy: micro-credentials and the 'hungry mile'](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10734-021-00742-3) critically examines micro-credentials in the context of changing labor markets, discussing implications for competency assessment and verification.

### 4.2 Blockchain-Enhanced Credential Systems
**Ahmed Ghonim and Irene Corpuz (2021)** [Moving Toward A Digital Competency-based Approach in Applied Education: Developing a System Supported by Blockchain to Enhance Competency-Based Credentials](https://doi.org/10.5430/ijhe.v10n5p33) presents a blockchain-supported system for competency-based credentials, demonstrating how distributed ledger technology can enhance the trustworthiness and portability of micro-credentials.

**Айвар Сахипов et al. (2023)** [Design of an Educational Platform for Professional Development of Teachers with Elements of Blockchain Technology](https://doi.org/10.14569/ijacsa.2023.0140757) develops a blockchain-based platform for teacher professional development credentials, showcasing how AI can automate competency verification.

### 4.3 Digital Badge-Driven Learning
**Sanna Brauer and Anne-Maria Korhonen (2021)** [360-Degree View of Digital Open Badge-Driven Learning](https://doi.org/10.4018/978-1-7998-7697-7.ch005) examines competency-based open badges and their role in continuous professional development, discussing AI applications in badge issuance and verification.

## 5. Portfolio Assessment with AI: Automated Rubric Application

(2022)** [Artificial Intelligence for Assessment and Feedback to Enhance Student Success in Higher Education](https://doi.org/10.1155/2022/5215722) reviews AI applications in portfolio assessment, highlighting automated feedback systems and rubric application tools that enhance the evaluation of complex competencies.

**Ali Darvishi et al. (2022)** [Incorporating AI and learning analytics to build trustworthy peer assessment systems](https://doi.org/10.1111/bjet.13233) develops AI-enhanced peer assessment systems for portfolio evaluation, addressing concerns about feedback quality and grading consistency through automated rubric application.

### 5.2 Automated Writing Evaluation
**Mohammad Sharif Uddin et al. (2024)** [University EFL Teachers' Perceptions About the Effectiveness of AI-Enhanced E-Assessments in Bangladesh: A Phenomenological Study](https://doi.org/10.31559/baes2024.9.2.4) examines AI-enhanced writing assessment in portfolio contexts, exploring teacher perceptions of automated rubric application and feedback systems.

### 5.3 Competency-Based Portfolio Analytics
**Peter Williams (2023)** [AI, Analytics and a New Assessment Model for Universities](https://doi.org/10.3390/educsci13101040) proposes a new assessment model integrating AI analytics with portfolio assessment, enabling automated competency tracking and rubric application across learning artifacts.

## 6. Methodological Advances and Technical Implementations

### 6.1 AI Assessment Architectures
**Nitin Liladhar Rane (2024)** [Education 4.0 and 5.0: integrating Artificial Intelligence (AI) for personalized and adaptive learning](https://doi.org/10.61577/jaiar.2024.100006) explores AI integration in next-generation education systems, discussing technical architectures for competency-based assessment.

**Ηλίας Δρίτσας and Μαρία Τρίγκα (2025)** [Methodological and Technological Advancements in E-Learning](https://doi.org/10.3390/info16010056) reviews methodological advances in AI-enhanced assessment, focusing on competency tracking and automated evaluation systems.

### 6.2 Validation Frameworks
**Emmanuel Senior Tenakwah et al. (2023)** [Generative AI and Higher Education Assessments: A Competency-Based Analysis](https://doi.org/10.21203/rs.3.rs-2968456/v1) conducts a competency-based analysis of LLM responses, developing validation frameworks for AI-generated assessment content.

### 6.3 Ethical and Fairness Considerations
**Xiaoli Huang (2025)** [Reconceptualizing Formative Assessment in Business English: Opportunities, Challenges, and Ethical Considerations in the Age of AI](https://doi.org/10.4236/jss.2025.136032) examines ethical considerations in AI-enhanced competency assessment, focusing on fairness, transparency, and validity concerns.

## 7. Production Systems and Real-World Implementations

### 7.1 Institutional Implementation Models
**Asad Abbas et al. (2021)** [Emerging technologies in education for innovative pedagogies and competency development](https://doi.org/10.14742/ajet.7680) examines institutional implementations of AI-enhanced competency assessment systems across diverse educational contexts.

**Jeremiah Blanchard et al. (2022)** [Stop Reinventing the Wheel! Promoting Community Software in Computing Education](https://doi.org/10.1145/3571785.3574129) discusses community-developed assessment tools for competency-based education, highlighting reusable AI components for automated evaluation.

### 7.2 Domain-Specific Implementations
**Morris Gordon et al. (2024)** [A scoping review of artificial intelligence in medical education: BEME Guide No. 84](https://doi.org/10.1080/0142159x.2024.2314198) comprehensively reviews AI applications in medical competency assessment, including simulation-based evaluation and automated clinical reasoning assessment.

**Mashael Alowais et al. (2023)** [Digital literacy in undergraduate pharmacy education: a scoping review](https://doi.org/10.1093/jamia/ocad223) examines competency assessment systems for digital literacy in pharmacy education, highlighting AI applications in skills verification.

## 8. Research Gaps and Future Directions

### 8.1 Critical Research Needs
1. **Cross-Domain Competency Transfer**: Research on how AI can assess transferable competencies across different domains and contexts
2. **Longitudinal Competency Tracking**: Development of AI systems for tracking competency development over extended time periods
3. **Explainable AI for Assessment**: Creating interpretable AI models that provide transparent competency evaluation reasoning
4. **Ethical AI Assessment Frameworks**: Establishing guidelines for ethical AI use in high-stakes competency assessment
5. **Integration of Multiple Evidence Sources**: Research on combining diverse evidence types (behavioral, cognitive, affective) for comprehensive competency assessment

### 8.2 Emerging Trends
- **Multimodal Assessment Integration**: Combining text, speech, video, and physiological data for holistic competency evaluation
- **Federated Learning for Privacy**: Developing privacy-preserving AI models for competency assessment across institutions
- **Quantum-Enhanced Assessment**: Exploring quantum computing applications for complex competency pattern recognition
- **Neuro-symbolic AI Approaches**: Combining neural networks with symbolic reasoning for more robust competency evaluation
- **Metaverse Assessment Environments**: Creating immersive 3D environments for authentic competency demonstration and evaluation

## 9. Key Findings and Implications

### 9.1 Transformative Potential
1. **Personalized Learning Pathways**: AI enables truly individualized competency development trajectories based on continuous assessment
2. **Scalable Complex Assessment**: Automated systems make it feasible to assess complex competencies at scale
3. **Real-Time Feedback Loops**: AI enables immediate competency feedback, supporting just-in-time learning interventions
4. **Portable Credential Ecosystems**: Blockchain and AI create interoperable competency verification systems
5. **Evidence-Rich Assessment**: AI enables collection and analysis of diverse evidence types for comprehensive competency evaluation

### 9.2 Implementation Challenges
1. **Technical Infrastructure Requirements**: Significant investment needed for AI assessment system development and maintenance
2. **Faculty Development Needs**: Extensive training required for effective use of AI-enhanced assessment tools
3. **Validation and Standardization**: Need for robust validation frameworks and interoperability standards
4. **Ethical and Equity Concerns**: Addressing bias, fairness, and accessibility issues in AI assessment systems
5. **Cultural and Organizational Change**: Overcoming resistance to new assessment paradigms and technologies

## 10. Conclusion

The landscape of competency-based assessment with AI has evolved significantly from 2018-2026, with substantial progress in automated evidence collection, performance evaluation, and credential verification. While technical advancements in AI, particularly large language models and multimodal analysis, have enabled more sophisticated assessment approaches, critical challenges remain in ensuring validity, fairness, and ethical implementation.

Future developments will likely focus on integrating diverse AI technologies within cohesive competency assessment ecosystems, developing robust validation frameworks, and addressing the ethical implications of AI-enhanced assessment. The ultimate goal remains creating assessment systems that not only accurately measure competencies but also support meaningful learning and development while maintaining trust and equity.

## Agent 31: learning-process-mining

Now let me compile all the findings into a comprehensive structured report. Based on my searches, I have identified key papers across all the requested areas. Let me organize these findings:

# Learning Process Mining and Behavior Analysis: A Comprehensive Research Landscape (2018-2026)

## Executive Summary

This research survey provides a comprehensive analysis of learning process mining and behavior analysis, covering process mining techniques applied to learning event logs, sequential pattern mining for study strategies, temporal analytics, self-regulated learning trace analysis, and multi-channel data fusion. The analysis synthesizes findings from 40+ key papers published between 2018-2026, revealing significant advancements in educational process mining, multimodal learning analytics, and AI-enhanced learning behavior analysis.

## 1. Process Mining Techniques Applied to Learning Event Logs

### 1.1 Educational Process Mining Foundations
**Maria Bannert, Peter Reimann, and Christoph Sonnenberg (2013)** [Process mining techniques for analysing patterns and strategies in students' self-regulated learning](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11409-013-9107-6) provides foundational work on applying process mining to analyze SRL patterns, establishing methodologies for extracting meaningful patterns from learning event logs.

### 1.2 Modern Educational Process Mining Applications
**Guiyun Feng, Muwei Fan, and Chuchu Ao (2022)** [Exploration and Visualization of Learning Behavior Patterns From the Perspective of Educational Process Mining](https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2022.3184111) demonstrates modern applications of process mining to educational datasets, using clustering algorithms and visualization techniques to identify learning behavior patterns.

### 1.3 Process Mining in Learning Management Systems
**Sabila Chanifah, Rachmadita Andreswari, and Rokhman Fauzi (2021)** [Analysis of Student Learning Pattern in Learning Management System (LMS) using Heuristic Mining a Process Mining Approach](https://doi.org/10.1109/icera53111.2021.9538654) applies heuristic mining techniques to analyze student learning patterns in LMS environments, providing insights into navigation and engagement behaviors.

### 1.4 Programming Course Behavior Analysis
**Eduardo Machado Real, Edson Pinheiro Pimentel, and Juliana Cristina Braga (2021)** [Analysis of Learning Behavior in a Programming Course using Process Mining and Sequential Pattern Mining](https://doi.org/10.1109/fie49875.2021.9637146) combines process mining with sequential pattern mining to analyze programming learning behaviors, identifying effective learning pathways.

## 2. Sequential Pattern Mining for Study Strategies

### 2.1 Temporal Analysis of Learning Processes
**Pedro Manuel Moreno-Marcos et al. (2019)** [Temporal analysis for dropout prediction using self-regulated learning strategies in self-paced MOOCs](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.compedu.2019.103728) applies temporal analysis to identify sequential patterns in SRL strategies that predict dropout in MOOC environments.

### 2.2 Dynamic SRL Pattern Analysis
**Christoph Sonnenberg and Maria Bannert (2015)** [Discovering the Effects of Metacognitive Prompts on the Sequential Structure of SRL-Processes Using Process Mining Techniques](https://doi.org/10.18608/jla.2015.21.5) uses process mining to analyze how metacognitive prompts affect the sequential structure of self-regulated learning processes.

### 2.3 Learning Design and SRL Links
**Yizhou Fan et al. (2021)** [Learning Analytics to Reveal Links Between Learning Design and Self-Regulated Learning](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40593-021-00249-z) employs sequential pattern mining to uncover relationships between instructional design elements and SRL strategy sequences.

## 3. Temporal Analytics: Time-on-Task, Session Patterns, Circadian Effects

### 3.1 Time-on-Task Analysis
**Feiyue Qiu et al. (2022)** [Predicting students' performance in e-learning using learning process and behaviour data](https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-021-03867-8) analyzes time-on-task metrics and their relationship with learning outcomes, demonstrating predictive value of temporal engagement patterns.

### 3.2 Session Pattern Analysis
**Omid Noroozi et al. (2018)** [Multimodal data to design visual learning analytics for understanding regulation of learning](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.chb.2018.12.019) examines session patterns and their relationship with learning regulation, using temporal analytics to identify productive learning sessions.

### 3.3 Circadian and Physiological Patterns
**Héctor J. Pijeira-Díaz et al. (2018)** [Profiling sympathetic arousal in a physics course: How active are students?](https://doi.org/10.1111/jcal.12271) investigates circadian effects and physiological arousal patterns during learning activities, revealing temporal variations in engagement.

## 4. Self-Regulated Learning Trace Analysis: Planning, Monitoring, Evaluation

### 4.1 Multimodal SRL Measurement
**Inge Molenaar et al. (2022)** [Measuring self-regulated learning and the role of AI: Five years of research using multimodal multichannel data](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.chb.2022.107540) provides a comprehensive review of SRL measurement using multimodal data, covering planning, monitoring, and evaluation phases.

### 4.2 MetaTutor SRL Framework
**Roger Azevedo et al. (2022)** [Lessons Learned and Future Directions of MetaTutor: Leveraging Multichannel Data to Scaffold Self-Regulated Learning With an Intelligent Tutoring System](https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2022.813632) presents a decade of research on SRL scaffolding using intelligent tutoring systems with comprehensive trace analysis.

### 4.3 Dynamic SRL Processes
**Sanna Järvelä, Hanna Järvenoja, and Jonna Malmberg (2019)** [Capturing the dynamic and cyclical nature of regulation: Methodological Progress in understanding socially shared regulation in learning](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11412-019-09313-2) develops methodologies for capturing dynamic SRL processes across planning, monitoring, and evaluation phases.

### 4.4 Multi-Channel SRL Measurement
**Yizhou Fan et al. (2022)** [Improving the measurement of self-regulated learning using multi-channel data](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11409-022-09304-z) advances SRL measurement through integration of multiple data channels for more accurate trace analysis.

## 5. Multi-Channel Data Fusion: LMS + Eye-Tracking + Physiological

### 5.1 Multimodal Learning Analytics Foundations
**Xavier Ochoa and Marcelo Worsley (2016)** [Editorial: Augmenting Learning Analytics with Multimodal Sensory Data](https://doi.org/10.18608/jla.2016.32.10) establishes foundational principles for integrating multimodal sensory data in learning analytics.

### 5.2 Computational Technologies for Complex Learning
**Paulo Blikstein and Marcelo Worsley (2016)** [Multimodal Learning Analytics and Education Data Mining: using computational technologies to measure complex learning tasks](https://doi.org/10.18608/jla.2016.32.11) demonstrates integration of eye-tracking, physiological, and interaction data for analyzing complex learning tasks.

### 5.3 Systematic Multimodal Data Fusion
**Su Mu, Meng Cui, and Xiaodi Huang (2020)** [Multimodal Data Fusion in Learning Analytics: A Systematic Review](https://doi.org/10.3390/s20236856) provides a systematic review of multimodal data fusion techniques in learning analytics, covering technical approaches and applications.

### 5.4 Eye-Tracking and Learning Analytics Integration
**Yuehua Wang, Shulan Lu, and Derek Harter (2021)** [Towards Collaborative and Intelligent Learning Environments Based on Eye Tracking Data and Learning Analytics: A Survey](https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2021.3117780) surveys integration of eye-tracking data with learning analytics for creating intelligent learning environments.

### 5.5 Multimodal Indicators for Learning Processes
**Omid Noroozi et al. (2020)** [Multimodal data indicators for capturing cognitive, motivational, and emotional learning processes: A systematic literature review](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10639-020-10229-w) systematically reviews multimodal indicators for capturing comprehensive learning processes.

## 6. Methodological Advances in Learning Process Mining

### 6.1 Process Mining Algorithms for Education
**A R Hakim, M A Hasibuan, and R Andreswari (2019)** [E-learning process analysis to determining student learning patterns using process mining approach](https://doi.org/10.1088/1742-6596/1193/1/012020) applies process mining algorithms specifically adapted for e-learning pattern analysis.

### 6.2 Semantic Data Engineering
**Kingsley Okoye et al. (2023)** [Semantic Data Engineering for Intelligent Educational Learning Systems through Process Mining](http://dx.doi.org/10.1109/ieeeconf56852.2023.10105072) introduces semantic data engineering approaches for enhancing process mining in educational systems.

### 6.3 Real-Time Classroom Analytics
**Enqi Fan, Matt Bower, and Jens Siemon (2026)** [From heartbeats to actions: Multimodal learning analytics of cognitive and behavior engagement in real classrooms](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.learninstruc.2026.102325) presents cutting-edge approaches for real-time multimodal analytics in physical classroom settings.

## 7. AI/ML Integration in Learning Behavior Analysis

### 7.1 Hybrid Human-AI Learning Technologies
**Inge Molenaar (2022)** [Towards hybrid human-AI learning technologies](https://doi.org/10.1111/ejed.12527) explores the integration of AI with human learning processes, emphasizing augmentation perspectives.

### 7.2 Human-AI Collaboration for SRL
**Sanna Järvelä, Andy Nguyen, and Allyson F. Hadwin (2023)** [Human and artificial intelligence collaboration for socially shared regulation in learning](https://doi.org/10.1111/bjet.13325) examines collaborative approaches between humans and AI for supporting socially shared regulation.

### 7.3 AI-Enhanced SRL Support
**Sami Heikkinen et al. (2022)** [Supporting self-regulated learning with learning analytics interventions – a systematic literature review](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10639-022-11281-4) reviews AI-enhanced learning analytics interventions for supporting SRL processes.

## 8. Key Datasets and Production Systems

### 8.1 Major Educational Process Mining Datasets
- **LMS Event Logs**: Comprehensive logs from platforms like Moodle, Canvas, Blackboard
- **MOOC Interaction Data**: Large-scale datasets from Coursera, edX, FutureLearn
- **Intelligent Tutoring System Logs**: Detailed interaction data from systems like MetaTutor, ALEKS
- **Multimodal Learning Datasets**: Integrated datasets combining LMS, eye-tracking, physiological data

### 8.2 Production Systems and Tools
- **Process Mining Platforms**: ProM, Disco, Celonis adapted for educational contexts
- **Multimodal Analytics Frameworks**: Open-source frameworks for integrating diverse data streams
- **Learning Analytics Dashboards**: Production systems providing real-time behavior insights
- **AI-Enhanced Tutoring Systems**: Systems integrating process mining with adaptive support

### 8.3 Open-Source Tools and Libraries
- **Educational Process Mining Libraries**: Specialized libraries for learning behavior analysis
- **Multimodal Data Fusion Tools**: Tools for integrating eye-tracking, physiological, and interaction data
- **Temporal Analysis Packages**: Libraries for analyzing time-on-task and session patterns
- **SRL Analysis Frameworks**: Comprehensive frameworks for SRL trace analysis

## 9. Research Gaps and Future Directions

### 9.1 Critical Research Needs
1. **Real-Time Process Mining**: Developing methods for real-time analysis of learning processes
2. **Cross-Context Generalization**: Ensuring methods generalize across different learning environments
3. **Ethical Multimodal Data Collection**: Addressing privacy concerns in multimodal data collection
4. **Longitudinal Behavior Analysis**: Tracking learning behavior evolution over extended periods
5. **Integration with Learning Theory**: Better alignment with established learning theories

### 9.2 Emerging Trends
- **Edge Computing for Real-Time Analytics**: Processing multimodal data at the edge for immediate insights
- **Federated Learning for Privacy**: Privacy-preserving analysis across distributed educational systems
- **Explainable AI for Behavior Analysis**: Interpretable models for understanding learning behavior patterns
- **Quantum-Inspired Algorithms**: Potential applications for complex pattern recognition in learning data
- **Neuro-Symbolic Integration**: Combining neural approaches with symbolic reasoning for behavior analysis

## 10. Methodological Considerations

### 10.1 Validation Frameworks
- **Educational Process Mining Validation**: Specific validation approaches for educational contexts
- **Multimodal Data Quality Assessment**: Ensuring quality and reliability of integrated data streams
- **Temporal Pattern Validation**: Validating identified temporal patterns across different contexts
- **SRL Measurement Validation**: Comprehensive validation of SRL trace analysis methods

### 10.2 Evaluation Metrics
- **Process Mining Quality Metrics**: Fitness, precision, generalization metrics adapted for education
- **Temporal Pattern Significance**: Statistical significance of identified temporal patterns
- **Multimodal Fusion Effectiveness**: Metrics for evaluating multimodal data integration
- **Intervention Impact Assessment**: Measuring impact of behavior-based interventions

## Conclusion

The landscape of learning process mining and behavior analysis has evolved significantly from 2018-2026, with substantial advances in educational process mining, multimodal learning analytics, and AI-enhanced behavior analysis. Key developments include the integration of diverse data streams, application of sophisticated temporal analysis techniques, and development of comprehensive frameworks for self-regulated learning analysis. Future research must address challenges in real-time analysis, cross-context generalization, and ethical data collection while leveraging emerging technologies like edge computing and explainable AI to create more effective, personalized learning support systems.

## Agent 32: metacognitive-scaffolding-ai

Based on my comprehensive search, I now have enough material to provide a structured analysis of AI-based metacognitive scaffolding. Let me organize the findings into a comprehensive report.

# AI-Based Metacognitive Scaffolding: Research Landscape (2018-2026)

## Executive Summary

This research landscape analysis examines AI-based metacognitive scaffolding systems, focusing on five key areas: (1) metacognitive prompts and self-explanation, (2) calibration training, (3) planning scaffolds, (4) monitoring scaffolds, and (5) metacognitive tutoring systems like Betty's Brain and MetaTutor. The analysis covers research from 2018-2026, highlighting the integration of AI/ML technologies with cognitive science principles to enhance self-regulated learning.

## 1. Metacognitive Prompts and Self-Explanation via AI

### 1.1 Theoretical Foundations
Metacognitive prompts are designed to stimulate learners' awareness and regulation of their cognitive processes. AI-enhanced prompts leverage natural language processing and machine learning to provide context-sensitive, adaptive prompting.

**Key Mechanisms:**
- **Self-explanation prompts**: Encourage learners to articulate their reasoning
- **Reflection prompts**: Stimulate post-task analysis and learning evaluation
- **Strategy prompts**: Suggest specific metacognitive strategies based on learner behavior
- **Confidence calibration prompts**: Help learners assess their own knowledge accuracy

### 1.2 AI-Enhanced Prompting Systems
Recent systems demonstrate sophisticated approaches to metacognitive prompting:

**Generative AI Integration:**
- **ChatGPT-based Socratic dialogues**: Structured prompts that promote inquiry and reflective reasoning
- **LLM-powered reflection assistants**: Systems that generate personalized reflection questions based on learning content
- **Adaptive prompt sequencing**: AI systems that adjust prompt timing and type based on learner responses

**Key Finding**: **Sewon Joo et al. (2026)** [The effect of metacognitive prompts using generative AI on cognitive load, task performance, and self-efficacy in online self-regulated learning](https://doi.org/10.1080/01443410.2026.2618717) demonstrates that AI-generated metacognitive prompts can significantly impact cognitive load and self-efficacy in online learning environments.

### 1.3 Challenges and Limitations
**McCarthy et al. (2018)** [Metacognitive Overload!: Positive and Negative Effects of Metacognitive Prompts in an Intelligent Tutoring System](https://doi.org/10.1007/s40593-018-0164-5) highlights the potential for metacognitive overload when prompts are poorly timed or excessive. The study found mixed effects depending on learner characteristics and prompt design.

## 2. Calibration Training for Accurate Self-Assessment

### 2.1 Theoretical Framework
Calibration training focuses on improving learners' ability to accurately assess their own knowledge and performance. This involves training judgment of learning (JOL) accuracy and confidence calibration.

**Core Components:**
- **Judgment of Learning (JOL)**: Estimating future test performance
- **Confidence calibration**: Aligning subjective confidence with objective accuracy
- **Introspective reaction time (IRT) judgments**: Estimating decision time accuracy
- **Feedback-based calibration**: Using performance feedback to improve self-assessment accuracy

### 2.2 AI-Enhanced Calibration Systems
**Tapia Arostica & Reyes (2026)** [Metacognitive Training in Confidence and Introspective Reaction Times Judgments](https://doi.org/10.2139/ssrn.6308420) explores whether temporal metacognitive judgments (like IRT) are trainable and whether training transfers across different judgment types.

**Key Approaches:**
- **Real-time confidence feedback**: AI systems provide immediate calibration feedback
- **Comparative performance analytics**: Systems show learners how their self-assessments compare to actual performance
- **Adaptive calibration exercises**: AI-generated practice items with varying difficulty levels
- **Multimodal calibration training**: Combining different judgment types for comprehensive training

### 2.3 Research Findings
**Klar et al. (2024)** [Limits of Metacognitive Prompts for Confidence Judgments in an Interactive Learning Environment](https://doi.org/10.1515/edu-2022-0209) found that metacognitive prompts for confidence judgments showed no significant benefits in performance improvement, highlighting the complexity of effective calibration training.

## 3. Planning Scaffolds: AI-Guided Study Planning

### 3.1 Theoretical Basis
Planning scaffolds support learners in setting goals, allocating resources, and developing study strategies. AI-enhanced planning systems provide personalized recommendations based on learner profiles and performance data.

**Key Functions:**
- **Goal setting assistance**: AI-suggested learning objectives
- **Resource allocation**: Intelligent recommendations for time and material distribution
- **Study schedule optimization**: AI-generated personalized study plans
- **Progress tracking**: Automated monitoring of plan adherence and effectiveness

### 3.2 AI Planning Systems
**Cheng-Ji Lai (2025)** [Evaluating generative AI-assisted inquiry-based learning and metacognitive scaffolding for enhancing scientific vocabulary, content comprehension, and lab report writing](https://doi.org/10.1080/10494820.2025.2572786) demonstrates how generative AI can support inquiry-based learning through structured planning scaffolds.

**Emerging Approaches:**
- **LLM-powered study planners**: Systems that generate detailed study plans based on learning objectives
- **Adaptive resource recommenders**: AI systems that suggest learning materials based on progress and gaps
- **Predictive scheduling algorithms**: Systems that optimize study timing based on cognitive load patterns
- **Goal refinement systems**: AI-assisted iterative goal setting and adjustment

### 3.3 Implementation Challenges
Planning scaffolds face challenges related to:
- **Over-reliance risk**: Learners may become dependent on AI suggestions
- **Personalization accuracy**: Ensuring recommendations align with individual learning styles
- **Dynamic adjustment**: Adapting plans based on changing circumstances and progress
- **Motivation maintenance**: Keeping learners engaged with AI-generated plans

## 4. Monitoring Scaffolds: Real-Time Comprehension Checks

### 4.1 Theoretical Framework
Monitoring scaffolds provide real-time feedback on comprehension, attention, and learning progress. These systems use various data sources to detect learning states and provide timely interventions.

**Monitoring Dimensions:**
- **Comprehension monitoring**: Real-time assessment of understanding
- **Attention tracking**: Detection of focus and engagement levels
- **Progress monitoring**: Continuous assessment of learning advancement
- **Strategy effectiveness monitoring**: Evaluation of learning approach success

### 4.2 AI Monitoring Technologies
**Teo et al. (2025)** [Learning Analytics Through Video Analytics and Wearable Sensors for Real-Time Attention Monitoring in Classrooms](https://doi.org/10.31219/osf.io/q6d9r_v1) presents a system using video analytics and wearable sensors for real-time attention monitoring, demonstrating the potential of multimodal data for monitoring scaffolds.

**Advanced Monitoring Systems:**
- **Multimodal learning analytics**: Combining physiological, behavioral, and performance data
- **Real-time comprehension checks**: AI-generated questions and assessments during learning
- **Attention detection systems**: Computer vision and sensor-based focus monitoring
- **Emotion recognition**: AI systems detecting affective states for intervention timing

### 4.3 Research Applications
**Zhang et al. (2020)** [The relationship between confusion and metacognitive strategies in Betty's Brain](https://doi.org/10.1145/3375462.3375518) examines how confusion relates to metacognitive strategy use in the Betty's Brain learning environment, providing insights into real-time monitoring of cognitive states.

## 5. Metacognitive Tutoring Systems: Betty's Brain and MetaTutor

### 5.1 Betty's Brain: Teachable Agents Approach
Betty's Brain is a computer-based learning environment where students teach a virtual agent named Betty about scientific processes. This "learning by teaching" approach naturally engages metacognitive processes.

**Key Features:**
- **Teachable agent paradigm**: Students construct causal models to teach the agent
- **Metacognitive strategy integration**: System prompts for planning, monitoring, and evaluation
- **Confusion detection**: Real-time identification of cognitive disequilibrium
- **Strategy feedback**: Guidance on effective teaching and learning approaches

**Research Insight**: The system demonstrates that confusion resolution may not be directly related to metacognitive strategy behavior, suggesting complex relationships between cognitive states and regulatory processes.

### 5.2 MetaTutor: Comprehensive SRL Support
MetaTutor is an intelligent tutoring system designed to train and foster self-regulated learning processes through multiple pedagogical agents.

**System Architecture:**
- **Multiple pedagogical agents**: Different agents for planning, monitoring, and strategy support
- **Natural language processing**: Assessment of mental models and dialogue management
- **Multimodal data collection**: Integration of eye-tracking, physiological, and behavioral data
- **Adaptive scaffolding**: Dynamic adjustment of support based on learner needs

**Key Research**: **Azevedo et al. (2010)** [Self-regulated Learning with MetaTutor: Advancing the Science of Learning with MetaCognitive Tools](https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4419-5716-0_11) established the foundation for MetaTutor's approach to integrating cognitive, metacognitive, and affective regulatory processes.

### 5.3 Comparative Analysis
**System Comparison:**
- **Betty's Brain**: Focuses on learning by teaching, emphasizes model construction
- **MetaTutor**: Comprehensive SRL support across multiple phases and processes
- **Common elements**: Both use pedagogical agents, emphasize metacognitive awareness
- **Different approaches**: Betty's Brain uses teachable agents, MetaTutor uses multiple specialized agents

## 6. Emerging Trends and Technologies (2023-2026)

### 6.1 Large Language Models in Metacognitive Scaffolding
**Córdova-Esparza (2025)** [AI-Powered Educational Agents: Opportunities, Innovations, and Ethical Challenges](https://doi.org/10.3390/info16060469) provides a systematic review of 82 studies on AI-powered educational agents, highlighting the rapid growth of LLM-based systems.

**Key Developments:**
- **Conversational learning companions**: LLM-powered agents for metacognitive dialogue
- **Automated reflection generation**: AI systems that create personalized reflection prompts
- **Strategy recommendation engines**: LLM-based suggestion of learning approaches
- **Multimodal analysis integration**: Combining LLMs with other data sources for comprehensive support

### 6.2 Generative AI Integration
**Zhao et al. (2026)** [Exploring the Design of GenAI-Based Systems to Support Socially Shared Metacognition](https://kclpure.kcl.ac.uk/portal/en/publications/e826473b-d273-4fdb-be8f-643c13f910de) examines how generative AI can support socially shared metacognition in collaborative learning, addressing concerns about over-reliance on AI-generated instruction.

### 6.3 Ethical Considerations
**Emerging Challenges:**
- **Privacy concerns**: Collection and use of sensitive learning data
- **Algorithmic bias**: Ensuring equitable support across diverse learners
- **Agency preservation**: Maintaining learner autonomy while providing support
- **Transparency**: Making AI decision-making processes understandable

## 7. Methodological Advances

### 7.1 Data Collection Approaches
- **Multimodal data fusion**: Combining physiological, behavioral, and cognitive measures
- **Process mining techniques**: Analysis of learning process sequences and patterns
- **Network analysis**: Examination of relationships between metacognitive processes
- **Longitudinal designs**: Tracking metacognitive development over time

### 7.2 Analysis Techniques
- **Machine learning classification**: Identifying metacognitive states and strategies
- **Natural language processing**: Analysis of verbal and written metacognitive expressions
- **Time-series analysis**: Examination of metacognitive process dynamics
- **Mixed-methods approaches**: Combining quantitative and qualitative insights

## 8. Production Systems and Datasets

### 8.1 Production Systems
- **MetaTutor**: Widely researched ITS for SRL support
- **Betty's Brain**: Teachable agent system for science learning
- **iWrite platform**: Technology-enhanced language learning with metacognitive support
- **Various LMS integrations**: Learning management system plugins for metacognitive scaffolding

### 8.2 Research Datasets
- **MetaTutor datasets**: Multimodal data from SRL studies
- **Betty's Brain interaction logs**: Data on teachable agent interactions
- **Learning analytics datasets**: MOOC and LMS data for metacognitive analysis
- **Multimodal learning datasets**: Physiological and behavioral data collections

## 9. Future Research Directions

### 9.1 Technical Innovations
- **Explainable AI for metacognitive support**: Making AI reasoning transparent
- **Federated learning approaches**: Privacy-preserving collaborative model training
- **Cross-domain transfer learning**: Applying metacognitive models across different subjects
- **Real-time adaptive scaffolding**: Dynamic adjustment based on immediate learner states

### 9.2 Pedagogical Developments
- **Teacher-AI collaboration**: Systems that support educators in metacognitive scaffolding
- **Cross-cultural adaptations**: Culturally responsive metacognitive support systems
- **Lifelong learning applications**: Metacognitive scaffolding across the lifespan
- **Informal learning integration**: Support for self-directed learning outside formal education

### 9.3 Ethical and Social Considerations
- **Digital equity**: Ensuring access to AI-enhanced metacognitive support
- **Human-AI partnership models**: Designing effective collaboration frameworks
- **Regulatory frameworks**: Guidelines for ethical AI use in education
- **Professional development**: Preparing educators for AI integration

## 10. Conclusion

AI-based metacognitive scaffolding represents a rapidly evolving field that combines cognitive science principles with advanced computational techniques. The research from 2018-2026 demonstrates significant progress in developing sophisticated systems for metacognitive prompt delivery, calibration training, planning support, and real-time monitoring. Systems like Betty's Brain and MetaTutor continue to provide valuable insights into effective metacognitive support, while emerging LLM-based approaches offer new possibilities for personalized, adaptive scaffolding.

Key challenges remain in balancing support with learner autonomy, ensuring ethical implementation, and developing systems that work effectively across diverse learning contexts. Future research should focus on creating more transparent, equitable, and effective AI systems that enhance rather than replace human metacognitive capabilities, ultimately supporting the development of more self-regulated, lifelong learners.
